Independent convergence — where actors adopt similar instruments without formal coordination — is evidenced by the structural match between the instruments themselves, not by documentation establishing a coordination relationship. This structural character entails the CC-A confidence level: the evidence demonstrates parallelism, not coordination intent. The qualifying criteria are calibrated accordingly: each criterion assesses similarity along a dimension the methodology considers analytically meaningful, and Criterion 1 serves as a mandatory gate.

Coordination evidence routing rule. When documentary coordination evidence exists between the actors of two candidate Parallel Policy events (bilateral agreements, joint statements, strategic partnerships, ministerial coordination mechanisms), the analyst assesses Types 1–6 first. If a documentary type is satisfied, the CC is classified under that type at the appropriate documentary confidence level. If no documentary type fits — because the instruments were independently developed through separate domestic processes despite the bilateral coordination environment — the CC remains Type 7 at CC-A. Domain-level coordination evidence is recorded in evidence_detail as analytical context but does not elevate the confidence level, because the CC determination itself remains analytical. This routing rule is the procedural complement to the floor = ceiling principle stated at §9.3.2.

Criterion Assessment
Mechanism Similarity Same policy tool type with documented sub-category convergence (the mechanism specificity sub-test; mandatory gate)
Industry Similarity Same industry per industry tagging system (Chapter 7)
Scope Similarity Comparable geographic/sectoral reach
Timing Overlap Events within domain-specific temporal window of each other (see §9.4.1)

Criterion 1 is a mandatory gate. If not met, no further assessment is conducted and the connection does not qualify.

Criterion 1: Mechanism Similarity

Events use the same policy tool category:

Category Includes Does NOT Include
Subsidy/Incentive Grants, tax credits, low-interest loans, production subsidies, investment incentives, matching funds Regulatory mandates, restrictions
Restriction/Control Export controls, import bans, entity lists, licensing requirements, technology transfer prohibitions, investment screening Incentive programmes, voluntary frameworks
Standard/Mandate Technical requirements, certification requirements, conformity assessment, mandatory standards, labelling requirements Voluntary guidelines, best practices
Framework/Governance Regulatory frameworks, governance structures, institutional arrangements, oversight mechanisms Individual awards, specific transactions
Investment/Buildout Infrastructure programmes, capacity expansion, facility construction, R&D investment programmes Operating restrictions, access controls

Events must fall within the same category row. Cross-category = criterion not met.

Clarification: Corporate product releases are excluded from Parallel Policy assessment under the framework-level scope limitation (§9.1.8). Corporate instruments that employ a policy tool — power purchase agreements, buildout programmes, procurement contracts — remain assessable under the mechanism categories.

Criterion 1 is a mandatory qualifying gate. Unlike Criteria 2, 3, and 4 — where any one may be unmet whilst the connection still qualifies at ≥3/4 — Criterion 1 must be met for the Parallel Policy assessment to proceed. If Criterion 1 is not met, the connection does not qualify and no further similarity assessment is conducted.

Mechanism Specificity Sub-Test. Category-level matching (both events falling within the same row of the mechanism table) is necessary but not sufficient. The analyst must additionally identify at least one documented sub-category characteristic — a specification, instrument sub-type, or structural feature finer-grained than the category label — shared by both events. The shared characteristic must be documented in the verified sources of both events. Where no sub-category characteristic can be identified from the verified source documentation, Criterion 1 is not met. The identified characteristic is recorded as specification_overlap in the mechanism assessment.

Sub-category characteristics vary by mechanism category. The following are indicative, not exhaustive:

Category Sub-category characteristics (indicative)
Subsidy/Incentive Instrument structure (grant, tax credit, production subsidy, matching fund); same industrial target; same fiscal mechanism; or comparable documented characteristic
Restriction/Control Instrument type (export control, entity list, investment screening, licensing requirement); same controlled-item class; same enforcement mechanism; or comparable documented characteristic
Standard/Mandate Instrument form (technical standard, certification requirement, conformity assessment, labelling requirement); same standards body class; same obligation structure; or comparable documented characteristic
Framework/Governance Governance instrument form (comprehensive risk-tiered regulation, sectoral governance framework, institutional establishment, oversight body); same institutional character (advisory vs enforcement); same mandate scope; or comparable documented characteristic
Investment/Buildout Hardware platform specification (architecture generation, networking standard), deployment model (sovereign procurement, commercial colocation, greenfield facility), institutional instrument type (procurement programme, investment fund, facility construction), same capacity class; or comparable documented characteristic

For Investment/Buildout, shared vendor alone does not satisfy the sub-test where the vendor holds dominant market share in the relevant technology segment. Where the proposed sub-category characteristic is vendor-related, at least one specification beyond the vendor name — architecture generation, networking standard, or deployment model — must be documented for both events to establish structural supply-chain interdependence rather than market ubiquity.

The sub-test does not require identical specifications. Events sharing the same instrument sub-type (e.g. both sovereign frontier supercomputers, both existing-reactor nuclear power purchase agreements, both national AI strategy frameworks with dedicated budgets) satisfy the test even where their internal architectures, scales, or operational details differ. The discriminator is whether a documented shared characteristic exists below the category level, not whether the events are identical.

Criterion 2: Industry Similarity

Events address the same industry, assessed through the industry tagging system (Chapter 7). Industry tags must be assigned to both events before Criterion 2 assessment.

Criterion 2 is met when the paired events share at least one industry tag — primary or secondary. Events with no shared industry tag do not meet industry similarity, regardless of thematic similarity in their strategic objectives.

Tag alignment Criterion status
Shared primary tag Met
Shared secondary tag (or one event’s primary matches the other’s secondary) Met
No shared tag Not met

Any industry-tag addition, removal, or reclassification on an event triggers Criterion 2 re-verification on every Parallel Policy connection incident to that event. Re-verification assesses whether the connection’s Criterion 2 determination — met or not met — still holds under the updated tag set. Where re-verification finds that a previously met Criterion 2 is no longer met because the shared tag has been removed or changed, the connection’s overall criterion count is recalculated; if the total falls below the ≥3/4 threshold, the connection is removed from the corpus. Where re-verification finds that a previously unmet Criterion 2 is now met because a tag addition creates a new shared tag, no automatic connection is created — new connections are entered only through the standard assessment process. A connection whose evidence basis has been removed is a data-integrity issue requiring immediate correction; a potential new connection is a discovery opportunity handled through normal operations.

Criterion 3: Scope Similarity

Events have comparable geographic reach and, where quantitative data is available, comparable financial or physical scale.

Scope similarity is assessed across two dimensions. Geographic scope must always be satisfied. Scale comparison applies only when quantitative data is available for both events.

Dimension 1 — Geographic Scope (always assessed):

Comparison Criterion Status
Both national-level Met
Both supranational (e.g., EU-level, ASEAN-level) Met
Both subnational (e.g., state/provincial) Met
National vs supranational Met
National vs subnational Not met

Dimension 2 — Scale Comparison (assessed when quantitative data is available for both events):

Where financial or physical capacity data is publicly available for both events, scale must be assessed using the highest applicable metric in the following hierarchy:

Priority Metric Applicability Threshold
Primary Financial scale (total programme value in common currency) Where financial data is publicly available for both events Within 3× of each other
Secondary Physical capacity (MW, GPU count, or equivalent physical unit) Where capacity data is available and financial data is unavailable or inapplicable for at least one event Within 3× of each other

If financial data is available for both events, the financial scale metric governs. If financial data is unavailable or inapplicable for at least one event, proceed to physical capacity.

Where the two events report financial values in different currencies, each value is converted to a common currency (USD) at the documented exchange rate contemporaneous to that event’s implementation date; where the resulting ratio falls within 10% of the 3× threshold, the exchange rate sensitivity is noted in the transparency note.

Scale Ratio Criterion Status
Within 3× of each other Met
Greater than 3× difference Not met

The 3× threshold is a strict binary pass/fail determination. No graduated assessment or margin of appreciation is applied.

Range-straddling provision: Where figures are reported as ranges and any point within the range exceeds the 3× threshold, the criterion is assessed as Not Met. The conservative bound governs.

Scale assessment status: Each scope assessment must record one of the following statuses for Dimension 2, stored as scale_assessment_status within the connection’s evidence_detail.criteria_assessment.scope:

Status Definition Outcome
Quantitative Financial or capacity data available for both events; ratio computed Pass or fail based on ratio
Inapplicable No financial or capacity metric exists by nature of the event types (regulatory instruments, governance frameworks, standards) Scope assessed on Dimension 1 alone
Indeterminate Financial or capacity data would be meaningful but is not publicly available for at least one event Scope assessed on Dimension 1 alone; status recorded for future review

Where Dimension 2 is inapplicable or indeterminate, the scope criterion is assessed on Dimension 1 (geographic scope) alone. This means events with undisclosed data face a less stringent scope assessment than events with disclosed data exceeding the 3× threshold. This asymmetry is inherent to evidence-based assessment and is documented here for transparency. The indeterminate status creates an audit trail: if data subsequently becomes available, the scope assessment for affected Coordination Connections is subject to review. Conversely, events assessed quantitatively carry richer analytical granularity in their connection records than events assessed on geographic scope alone.

Structural comparability override: Where two events employ fundamentally different mechanisms such that a scale ratio comparison would be misleading, scope may be assessed as Not Met even if the ratio falls within 3×. The rationale is documented in the evidence record.

Criterion 4: Timing Overlap

Events occur within the domain-specific Parallel Policy temporal window of each other (see §9.4.1).

Domain Window Criterion Status if Within Criterion Status if Outside
Energy/Infrastructure 24 months Met Not met
Security 6 months Met Not met
Technology 12 months Met Not met
Trade 12 months Met Not met

Implementation dates and cross-domain window assignment are governed by the universal temporal provisions (§9.4.1).

Threshold

Criterion 1 is assessed first. If not met, no further assessment is conducted and the connection does not qualify.

If Criterion 1 is met, the remaining criteria (2, 3, 4) are assessed. Each criterion scores 1 point if met, 0 if not.

Total points (of 4) Classification
4 Parallel Policy (CC-A)
3 (Criterion 1 + ≥2 of Criteria 2, 3, 4) Parallel Policy (CC-A)
2 or fewer No connection documented

For all Parallel Policy classifications, Analyst Notes must document the assessment for each criterion with specific evidence, including the sub-category characteristic(s) identified for Criterion 1 and the shared industry tag(s) for Criterion 2.

Scope Comparator: Distinct Actors

The ≥3/4 similarity threshold for Parallel Policy (Type 7) presupposes that the events being compared involve distinct actors. Two instruments issued by the same actor cannot constitute Parallel Policy — they are sequential policy by a single actor, not convergent policy by separate actors. The distinct actor requirement (§9.1.6) applies before the similarity assessment begins.

9.5 Bloc-Specific Verification Guide

9.5.1 Western Bloc Verification

Primary Source Locations:

Source Type Location Connection Types
Legislative citations Federal Register, Official Gazettes Policy Cascade, Regulatory Enablement
Budget allocations OMB, Treasury, Congressional appropriations Funding Flow
Joint statements State Department, Foreign Ministry releases Joint Issuance
Standards references ANSI, ISO, NIST publications Framework Family

Citation Patterns:

Phrase Indicates
“Pursuant to” Policy Cascade (verify via Authority Hierarchy, §9.2.1)
“In accordance with” Policy Cascade (verify via Authority Hierarchy, §9.2.1)
“Implementing” Policy Cascade (verify via Authority Hierarchy, §9.2.1)
“As required by” Policy Cascade (verify via Authority Hierarchy, §9.2.1)
“Authorised under” Regulatory Enablement
“In response to” Response
“Joint Statement by” Joint Issuance

Note: Linguistic markers are strong indicators but are not independently deterministic. Where Authority Level hierarchy between source and target events is unclear (same-level or adjacent-level), apply the Authority Hierarchy decision rules (§9.2.1) to confirm classification.

9.5.2 Eastern Bloc Verification

Primary Source Locations:

Source Type Location Connection Types
State Council documents english.www.gov.cn, State Council Gazette Policy Cascade
Ministry announcements MIIT, CAC, NDRC official sites Policy Cascade, Regulatory Enablement
Joint issuance documents Multi-ministry headers Joint Issuance
1+N framework documents NDRC, sectoral plans Policy Cascade, Framework Family

Chinese Linguistic Markers:

Chinese Pinyin English Connection Type
根据 gēnjù According to / Based on Context-dependent (§9.2.1 Note)
落实 luòshí Implement / Put into effect Policy Cascade
依照 yīzhào In accordance with Regulatory Enablement
贯彻 guànchè Carry out / Implement Policy Cascade
配套 pèitào Supporting / Complementary Framework Family
联合 liánhé Joint / Coordinated Joint Issuance
为了 wèile In order to / For (Intent marker — verify further)
针对 zhēnduì In response to / Targeting Response

Chinese Standards Body Verification (SAC/TC260):

Chinese national standards (GB and GB/T series) developed under TC260 use a normative reference architecture functionally equivalent to ISO/IEC Section 2. The 规范性引用文件 (Normative Reference Documents) section identifies standards whose content is incorporated by reference and constitutes binding requirements. The binding formula is “构成…必不可少的条款” (“constitutes an indispensable clause”).

When verifying Framework Family connections between Chinese GB/T AI standards:

  • Check the 规范性引用文件 section for normative cross-references — this is the equivalent of ISO/IEC Section 2 / Clause 2
  • The TC260 AI standards ecosystem exhibits a decentralised mesh structure with domain-specific cluster hubs (e.g., data annotation, large models, federated learning) rather than a single terminological anchor standard
  • GB (mandatory) and GB/T (recommended) standards may be structurally linked where the GB/T standard’s 规范性引用文件 section normatively references the parent GB standard
  • As with ISO/IEC, informative references, bibliography entries, and body-text mentions do not qualify as normative cross-references

1+N Framework Verification:

When verifying Framework Family connections in Chinese 1+N regulatory structures, check whether the “N” document (ministry regulation, provincial plan) cites the “1” directive (State Council document) by specific document number (e.g., “国发〔2017〕35号” / “Guo Fa [2017] No. 35”) in its operative provisions or legal basis section. Thematic alignment with national policy direction alone does not constitute a Framework Family connection — the textual citation must be present in the child document.

Where an “N” document cites enabling legislation (e.g., Cybersecurity Law, Data Security Law) rather than the State Council directive itself, this constitutes a separate connection chain (enabling law → implementing regulation), not a direct Framework Family link to the strategic plan.

Joint Issuance Document Identification:

Chinese joint issuance documents typically display:

  • Multiple ministry names in document header
  • Document number format indicating joint authorship
  • Publication in State Council Gazette with multi-agency attribution

Example Format:

国家发展改革委 工业和信息化部 财政部 关于...的通知
(Notice on... from NDRC, MIIT, Ministry of Finance)

9.6 Verification Process

9.6.1 Connection Identification Workflow

Step 1: Identify Candidate Connection
        ↓
        Candidate identified through:
        - Document review during event verification
        - Cross-reference in existing events
        - Systematic database review
        ↓
Step 2: Determine Connection Type
        ↓
        Apply definitions from §9.2.1
        ↓
Step 3: Locate Evidence
        ↓
        Find sources meeting Confidence Level requirements
        ↓
Step 4: Verify Temporal Window
        ↓
        Confirm events within applicable threshold (domain or Response Band)
        ↓
Step 5: Document Connection
        ↓
        Complete connection record format
        ↓
Step 6: Quality Review
        ↓
        Verify evidence supports claimed type and tier
        ↓
APPROVED CONNECTION

9.6.2 Quality Checklist

Before finalising a connection record:

9.6.3 Processing Sequence Integration

Connection identification occurs after all candidate events complete the Stages 1-4 processing pipeline and industry tag assignment. Unlike Stages 1-4 which assess individual events independently, Coordination Connections analysis identifies relationships across the verified event database.

Processing Sequence:

Stages 1-4: Individual Event Processing (per event)
    ↓
All Events Verified and Classified
    ↓
Chapter 9: Coordination Connections Analysis (cross-event)
    ↓
Connection Records Added to Event Data

Connections do not affect event verification status but provide additional analytical context for pattern analysis.

9.7 Limitations and Caveats

9.7.1 What CC Methodology Cannot Capture

Limitation Description
Undocumented coordination Hidden strategic intent, private communications
Informal influence Soft power, diplomatic pressure without documentation
Statistical correlation Temporal co-occurrence without documented link
Future intentions Planned but unimplemented connections
Classified relationships Intelligence or military cooperation without public documentation

9.7.2 Epistemic Boundaries

CC Methodology documents what is observable, not what is true.

A documented connection establishes that official sources claim a relationship exists. It does not guarantee the relationship operates as claimed or produces intended effects.

9.7.3 Verified vs. Analytical Connections

Level Status Use
CC-V, CC-E Verified/Established Full confidence in database outputs
CC-A Analytical Pattern recognition; flagged as analyst assessment

CC-A connections (Parallel Policy) are explicitly marked as analytical assessments, not documentary verification.

9.7.4 Three-Layer Analytical Structure

The CC taxonomy produces three analytically distinct layers, each with a different evidential character. The documentary-mechanics layer (Types 1–5) documents one event’s instrument acting on, enabling, funding, responding to, or cascading from another, evidenced by documentary citation or official statement at CC-V or CC-E confidence. The structural-membership layer (Type 6, Framework Family) documents shared participation in a common framework through parent instruments at CC-V confidence. The analytical-convergence layer (Type 7, Parallel Policy) assesses independently adopted parallel policies through the four-criterion similarity rubric (§9.4.3) at CC-A confidence (§9.3.2).

End of Chapter 9: Coordination Connections

Chapter 10: Infrastructure Threads

10.1 Purpose and Scope

10.1.1 Definition

An Infrastructure Thread is a factual cross-event link between a verified WEO event whose documented data names an AI infrastructure asset — a chip or accelerator, an energy source, or a fabrication facility — and the verified WEO event that records that asset’s production, creation, or supply. A thread asserts a single material fact: that the asset named in the first event is the asset the second event records.

A thread connects the event that produces, creates, or supplies an asset with the events documenting its deployment, its use in training, the power it supplies, or the chips it fabricates. It carries no assessment of relationship and no confidence grade.

Schema note: Threads are recorded in the source event’s infrastructureThreads array; each thread carries targetEventId, linkType, asset, and field, with source reserved for the unnamed-asset case (§10.3.3).

10.1.2 Relationship to WEO Methodology

Like Coordination Connections (Chapter 9), Infrastructure Threads are a supplementary layer recorded on top of WEO’s verified event database. Events must first pass the standard verification process (Chapter 3) before a thread may link them.

Unlike the five-stage sequence (§2.1), thread creation involves no qualification, significance assessment, tier classification, or domain classification. Threads are established by factual matching across the corpus — at event entry and through periodic corpus passes — outside the five-stage sequence and outside the Coordination Connection framework.

10.1.3 Status: Not a Coordination Connection

Infrastructure Threads sit outside Chapter 9. A thread is not a Coordination Connection: it is not assigned a connection type (Types 1–7, §9.2), and it is not graded CC-V, CC-E, or CC-A (§9.3.1). The thread layer carries none of the Coordination Connection apparatus — no typed relationship, no confidence classification, no criteria assessment. The full contrast is set out at §10.4.

10.2 Link Types

Every thread is one of four link types, each naming a specific material relationship between an asset and an event.

Link type Material fact recorded Source event (its field names the asset) Target event (the asset’s origin)
fabricated_at a chip is fabricated at a named foundry a chip event whose field names the foundry that fabricates it the foundry event
deploys a facility deploys a named chip or accelerator a facility event whose field names the accelerator it runs the chip event
trains_on a model is trained on a named chip or accelerator a model event whose field names the accelerator it trains on the chip event
powered_by a facility is powered by a named energy source a facility event whose field names the energy source that powers it the energy event

Reading a thread. Each link type is read as a plain statement from source to target — [source event] [link type] [target event]. For example, a deploys thread reads “this facility deploys this chip.”

Direction. A thread is directional. The source is the event whose documented field names the asset; the field pointer (§10.3.3) locates that reference. The target is the event that records the asset’s production, creation, or supply. Across the four types the layer runs from production, creation, and supply at one end to deployment and use at the other, and may be traversed from either end: from an asset’s origin event to every event threaded to it, or from a consuming event to every origin event it is threaded to.

Record. A thread is recorded on the source event as a minimal entry carrying none of the assessment apparatus of the connection record (§9.3.3):

asset:           [the specific asset name creating the link]
linkType:        [fabricated_at / deploys / trains_on / powered_by]
targetEventId:   [WEO Event ID of the asset's origin event]
field:           [the field of the source event that names the asset]

10.3 Verification Discipline

A thread is established only when documentary evidence records the specific material fact. Factual matching is not analytical inference: temporal proximity, thematic similarity, or a general relationship between two parties do not establish a thread.

10.3.1 The Claim Is the Material Fact

The claim a thread records is the narrow material fact — for example, “NVIDIA H100 GPUs, fabricated at TSMC.” A partnership or supply announcement can document that fact; the thread’s claim is the material fact the announcement records, not the agreement it announces. “Signed a partnership” is not an acceptable thread claim. A thread records what is physically true of an asset, not what two parties have agreed.

10.3.2 The Specific Link, Not the General Relationship

Where the only document asserts a general partnership without naming the fabrication, the deployment, or the supply, the material fact is not documented and no thread is established — “this chip is made there” would be an inference. The asset and its specific role must be named in the evidence.

The matching standard for the asset is a distinctive designation — an identifier that ties the reference to a specific asset. For chips and accelerators this is the model designation (e.g. H100, MI250X, S60): generic product categories do not satisfy it, and a vendor name alone does not identify a specific product. An architecture-generation designation (e.g. Blackwell) satisfies the standard only where the pointed field documents no finer designation. For fabrication facilities and energy sources it is the named facility, named operator, or named source as the origin event records it. Entity-level foundry designations (e.g. TSMC, Samsung Foundry) satisfy the standard where the origin event records the fabrication relationship at entity level. The designation must appear in the pointed field; where the pointed field carries none, the asset is unnamed and §10.3.3 applies.

10.3.3 Provenance

Provenance is carried by the source event’s documented data. The field pointer records which field of the source event names the asset, so that the thread rests on an existing, already-sourced fact rather than a fresh assertion.

  • Named asset (no fresh documentation required). Where the source event’s pointed field carries the asset’s distinctive designation — for example, an event whose description documents “NVIDIA H100 GPUs” — the thread points to that field and inherits its existing source.
  • Unnamed asset (not threadable as documented). Where an event documents an asset only generically — for example, “AI acceleration cards” — tying that asset to a specific product is a fresh claim, and a thread is not established until the source event’s own documented data names the specific asset. The identity is established on the event through post-qualification verification (Chapter 3, §3.15); the thread then points at the strengthened field. An unnamed asset’s identity is never asserted without a source.

10.3.4 Binary Status

A thread is binary: either the material fact is documented, in which case the thread exists, or it is not, in which case there is no thread. There is no graded confidence and no partial establishment.

10.4 Distinction from Coordination Connections

Infrastructure Threads and Coordination Connections are different kinds of object.

Attribute Coordination Connection (Chapter 9) Infrastructure Thread (Chapter 10)
What it documents A relationship between two verified events (§9.1.2) A material fact: an asset matched to its origin event (§10.1.1)
Typology One of seven connection types (§9.2) One of four link types — material, not relational
Confidence Graded CC-V / CC-E / CC-A (§9.3.1) None — verified or not (§10.3.4)
Assessment Typed criteria, evidence grading, analyst judgement Binary factual match; no assessment
Actor relationship Distinct actors required (§9.1.6) Actor-agnostic — same or distinct actors alike
Established by Stage 5 cross-database analysis (§2.1) Factual matching at event entry and through corpus passes, outside the five-stage sequence

A Coordination Connection documents a relationship between two verified events involving distinct actors (§9.1.2, §9.1.6). An Infrastructure Thread documents that the asset named in one event is the asset whose origin another records.

Threads are actor-agnostic. The Distinct Actor Requirement (§9.1.6) governs Coordination Connections and does not apply to threads. A thread makes no claim about actors: it may link two events involving the same actor — for example, a company’s chip event and that same company’s data-centre event (a deploys thread). An event pair that could not carry a Coordination Connection under §9.1.6 may carry a valid thread.

10.5 Limitations

  • Presence, not provenance of supply. A thread records that a named asset is present in an event and matched to its origin — not the commercial arrangement behind that presence. A deploys thread states that a facility uses a named chip — not that the chip’s manufacturer supplied it directly or under any particular contract.
  • Dependence on disclosure. An asset an event does not name cannot be threaded. The Structural Visibility Asymmetry of §4.11 applies: better-documented facilities will show denser threading than equally significant but less transparent ones. Thread density reflects disclosure, not importance.
  • Dependence on corpus coverage. A thread requires the asset’s origin event to exist as a verified event: an asset named in an event cannot be threaded until the corpus records the asset’s production, creation, or supply. Thread absence may therefore reflect corpus coverage rather than the absence of a material link.
  • Not a coordination signal. A thread is not evidence of coordination. Counts of threads measure the density of documented material links across the corpus, not the intensity of coordination.

End of Chapter 10: Infrastructure Threads
